Beyond Imagination: Objection about Place and the Rank of Perplexity
Answers objection about place and explains hayrah as faith in the unseen.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the rational response to the objection that God must occupy place, and why perplexity (hayrah) is the highest rank of faith.

Core Idea
God cannot be imagined, but He is known through certainty and perplexity.

After establishing Allah's self-subsistence, an objection arises: how can we conceive of a being without place? Al-Buti provides a nuanced response rooted in the limits of human imagination and the distinction between contingent and necessary existence. He then elevates perplexity (hayrah) as a genuine response to the divine mystery, matching our perplexity about our own soul and intellect.

Guided Path
1
Read the objection and answer
Read the objection carefully: 'How can I understand that there is no place for Allah?' Then read the answer that distinguishes contingent things from the Necessary Existent.
Notice the use of 'istiqrā'' (induction) in the objection.
2
Understand why analogy fails
The author rejects analogy (qiyās) because there is no common cause linking contingent and necessary existence. Ask yourself why this is a logical error.
3
Grasp the imagination argument
Imagination only captures sensory experiences; God is not sensible. Reflect on the example: if we could imagine God in a place, our intellect would be greater than God's, which is absurd.
This argument highlights the limits of human cognition.
4
Reflect on hayrah as faith
The passage concludes that perplexity (hayrah) after certainty is the highest rank of faith. Compare to our perplexity about the soul, intellect, and life. How does this connect to 'faith in the unseen'?

If you say: How can I understand that there is no place for Allah, while I know that there is no existent except that it occupies a place? The answer is that this knowledge of yours is derived only from surveying the states of bodies and contingent things. The attributes that clothe possible and contingent things do not necessarily clothe the Necessary Existent as well. And if you attempt analogy, it is an analogy without proof, for there is no common cause linking the original case and the branch. Rather, the intellect necessitates the difference of the Necessary Existent from all possibles in all that. And it does not harm you, after knowing this, that you cannot imagine in your mind His lack of occupying a place, glorified and exalted be He, because you have known that imagination is nothing but a mirror in which images of visible things that have passed through your senses are fixed. And this is something that has not passed through any of your senses, so how could you imagine it and conceive it? And it is not too much for you that your perplexity in conceiving the Divine Essence reaches the level of your perplexity about your own intellect and soul and the energy that Allah, exalted be He, made a secret upon which the existence of most of the existents you see around you is based. For where is the place of the intellect or the soul in your body? Where is the seat of life in living things? And what is its reality? You do not know, and no one knows the answer, despite everyone's certainty of the existence of intellect, soul, and life. Indeed, perplexity before these matters is a necessity resulting from the intellect being limited by the limits that the Creator, majestic is His majesty, willed for it. And how could the created not be perplexed when attempting to analyze and conceive its Creator? For this reason, perplexity – after faith in Him and His attributes, glorified and exalted be He – is the highest rank of faith. It suffices you that you are certain of His existence, and then be perplexed in understanding and conceiving Him. That is the reality of (faith in the unseen) which Allah commanded His servants. That is, that they believe in what is hidden from their senses and from their intellects in terms of limiting and qualifying this.
Source pages p. 115

Commentary
This passage defines the attribute al-qiyām bi al-dhāt (self-subsistence) and addresses an objection about God's relation to place. The author defines it as Allah's independence from any creator or substratum; He existed before time and space. The transmitted proof is the verse 'Allah is al-Ṣamad' (the Self-Sufficient), and the rational proof follows from His being the Necessary Existent, independent and unaffected. An objection is raised: how can we understand that God has no place when every existent we know occupies place? The answer distinguishes between contingent things and the Necessary Existent; attributes of possibles do not necessarily apply to God. Analogy is invalid because there is no common cause. The author then explains that imagination is limited to sensory images, and since God is not sensed, He cannot be imagined. If we could imagine Him, our intellect would be greater than His, which is absurd. So the intellect must be certain but not imagine; it is natural to be perplexed (hayrah). This perplexity is compared to our perplexity about the nature of our own intellect, soul, and life. Such perplexity, after faith, is the highest rank of faith. This is the essence of faith in the unseen (al-ghayb): belief in what is beyond senses and intellectual delimitation.

Key Terms
الحيرة
الإيمان بالغيب
القياس

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: God must occupy a place because all existents occupy place.
Correction: God is not a contingent being; His attributes differ from creation. Imagination is limited to sensory things, and God is beyond senses.

Checkpoint
Why does al-Buti consider hayrah (perplexity) after faith the highest rank of faith?
A) Because it proves the intellect is weak
B) Because it combines certainty with humility before divine transcendence
C) Because it is a form of worship
D) Because it is easy to achieve

Key Takeaway
Certainty in God's existence combined with perplexity in understanding His essence is the essence of faith in the unseen.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you try to think about Allah, do you find yourself automatically imagining something—even if you know it's wrong? How does this lesson challenge you to stop relying on your imagination and instead rest in certainty without images?
?
You are certain that your soul exists and that you have life, yet you cannot locate or fully define them. Does this realization humble you? How can you use this awareness to deepen your trust in Allah's revelation about Himself, especially regarding attributes that elude your grasp?
?
If perplexity is the highest rank of faith, then your discomfort with not being able to 'picture' God might be a sign of growing faith, not a lack of it. Does this truth give you peace or make you more restless? What would it mean for your prayer life if you stopped trying to visualize Allah and instead focused on His presence without form?

Divine Attributes
beginner

The Attribute of Dissimilarity to Created Things and Its Proofs
Defines the attribute and provides rational and textual proofs.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the meaning of Allah's attribute of dissimilarity to created things and the rational and Qur'anic proofs that establish it.

Core Idea
Allah is absolutely unlike anything in creation, proven by reason and revelation.

This lesson introduces the sixth divine attribute: al-Mukhalafatu lil-Hawadith (dissimilarity to created things). The author clarifies that Allah does not resemble any created being, then provides two distinct proofs: a rational argument based on the necessary implications of divinity, and a textual proof from two Qur'anic verses that unambiguously negate any likeness to Allah.

Guided Path
1
Read the definition of the attribute
The passage begins by explaining the meaning of al-Mukhalafatu lil-Hawadith: Allah does not resemble any created thing. Notice the specific negations: not a body, accident, universal, or particular.
This sets the foundation for understanding divine transcendence.
2
Understand the rational proof
The rational proof is based on 'al-luzum al-bayyin' (obvious implication). Divinity necessarily requires freedom from imperfections, and imperfections arise from being created and dependent. Therefore, the Necessary Being must be unlike all contingent beings.
This is a central argument in kalam for divine simplicity.
3
Examine the textual proof from the Qur'an
Two verses are cited: 'There is nothing like unto Him' (42:11) and 'And there is none comparable to Him' (112:4). Note that the particle 'ka' is added to 'mithl' for emphasis, strongly negating any likeness.
These verses are clear and decisive.
4
Reflect on the implication
This attribute guards against any anthropomorphism or comparison of Allah to creation. It also implies that all perfections belong to Allah in a way befitting Him, while imperfections are excluded.
Dissimilarity does not mean unknowability; it means transcendence.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ه - ( المخالفة للحوادث ( ومعناهـا عـدم مماثلته جل جلاله لها . فهو سبحانه وتعالى ليس بجرم ولا عرض ولا كلي ولا جزئي كما مر بيانه ، ولذلك فهو منزه عما تستلزمه هذه الصفات أيضاً من مختلف الصفات والأحوال والعوارض الجزئية التي تعتور الإنسان وغيره من الكائنات الأخرى ، كالنوم والغفلة والجوع والعطش والحاجة والعوارض النفسية والجسمية وما إلى ذلك
وقد ثبت برهان هذه الصفة الله تعالى بكل من دليلي العقل والنقل . أما دليل العقل فهو اللزوم البين بالمعنى الخاص . إذ الألوهية تستلزم البعد عن سائر النقائص ومن أبرز مظاهر النقص ما تتلبس به الحوادث من الصفات التي هي في الحقيقة ليست إلا نتيجة حدوثها وحاجتها إلى الموجد والمخصص . وأما دليل النقل فقوله تعالى : ﴿ لَيْسَ كَمِثْلِهِ شَيْءٌ وَهوَ السَّمِيعُ البَصِيرُ ﴾ [ الشورى : ١١ ] وإدخال كاف التشبيه على لفظ المثل مبالغة في نفي الشبيه والمثل الله تعالى .
ومثله قوله جل جلاله : ﴿ وَلَمْ يَكُنْ لَهُ كُفُواً أحد والكفؤ والمماثل واحد
H - (Dissimilarity to created things) and its meaning is that He, may His majesty be exalted, does not resemble them. So He, glorified and exalted be He, is not a body, nor an accident, nor a universal, nor a particular, as its explanation has passed. Therefore, He is free from what these attributes also entail of various attributes, states, and particular accidents that afflict humans and other beings, such as sleep, heedlessness, hunger, thirst, need, and psychological and physical accidents, and so on. And the proof of this attribute of Allah Almighty has been established by both rational and transmitted evidence. As for the rational evidence, it is the obvious implication (al-luzum al-bayyin) in the specific sense. For divinity necessitates distance from all imperfections, and among the most prominent manifestations of imperfection are the attributes that accidents (al-hawadith) are characterized by, which in reality are only a result of their origination (huduth) and their need for the Creator and the Specifier. As for the transmitted evidence, it is His saying, the Most High: 'There is nothing like unto Him, and He is the All-Hearing, the All-Seeing' [al-Shura 42:11], and the introduction of the simile particle (kaf) onto the word 'mithl' is an exaggeration in negating any likeness or similarity to Allah Almighty. And similar to it is His saying, may His majesty be exalted: 'And there is none comparable to Him' [al-Ikhlas 112:4], and al-kufu' and al-mumathil are the same.
Source pages p. 117

Commentary
This passage defines the sixth attribute, al-mukhalafatu lil-hawadith (dissimilarity to created things). It first explains the meaning: Allah does not resemble any created being. He is not a body (jism), accident (arad), universal (kulli), or particular (juz'i), as previously explained. Consequently, He is free from all imperfections that characterize created beings, such as sleep, heedlessness, hunger, thirst, need, and psychological or physical accidents. The proof for this attribute is established through both reason and revelation. The rational proof is the 'obvious implication' (al-luzum al-bayyin bi al-ma'na al-khass): divinity necessarily requires being free from all imperfections; contingent beings (hawadith) possess imperfections precisely because they are originated and dependent on a creator and specifier. Therefore, the divinity of the necessary being excludes such imperfections. The transmitted proof is the Qur'anic verse 'There is nothing like unto Him' (42:11), where the particle 'ka' is added to 'mithl' (likeness) to emphasize the negation of any similarity. The author also cites 'And there is none comparable to Him' (112:4), noting that 'kufu' and 'mumathil' are synonymous.

Key Terms
المخالفة للحوادث
اللزوم البين بالمعنى الخاص
الكفؤ
المثل

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some might think that 'dissimilarity' means complete otherness such that Allah is unknowable or that attributes like hearing and seeing are metaphorical.
Correction: The passage clarifies that dissimilarity specifically negates imperfections while affirming attributes of perfection in a manner befitting Allah, as seen in the same verse affirming 'He is the All-Hearing, the All-Seeing'.

Checkpoint
What is the rational proof (dalil al-'aql) given for al-mukhalafatu lil-hawadith?
A) The obvious implication that divinity necessitates distance from all imperfections.
B) The impossibility of seeing Allah in this world.
C) The fact that Allah is not a body or accident.
D) The Qur'anic verse 'There is nothing like unto Him'.

Key Takeaway
Allah is completely unlike any created being, a truth affirmed by reason and revelation.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you think of Allah's attributes like 'seeing' or 'hearing,' do you unconsciously imagine them as human-like? What specific mental habit will you adopt to truly affirm these attributes while negating any resemblance to creation?
?
The passage mentions that imperfections like sleep, heedlessness, and need are results of being created. Which of these do you find yourself most prone to forget about Allah? How does this forgetfulness affect your reliance on Him?
?
The rational proof shows that Allah is free from all need. Is there an area of your life where you still act as if Allah needs your worship or obedience? How will you internalize that He is completely independent, and your worship is only for your own benefit?

Objection on Shared Attributes Between Humans and Allah and the Answer
Raises an objection about shared attributes and begins the answer by classifying them into two types.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the objection that shared attributes seem to contradict Allah's dissimilarity to contingent things, and the beginning of the answer which distinguishes between attributes that arise from createdness and those that are not.

Core Idea
Shared names do not imply shared reality; many human attributes are rooted in contingency.

A natural question arises: if Allah is dissimilar to created beings, how can humans share attributes like knowledge and power with Him? The objection is answered by distinguishing two types of attributes: those that are inherently tied to createdness and those that are not. This lesson presents the objection and the first type.

Guided Path
1
Read the objection
Read the Arabic and translation to grasp the objection: shared attributes seem to contradict Allah's dissimilarity.
The objection is a common one among theologians.
2
Analyze the answer's structure
Notice that the answer begins by classifying human attributes into two types. Only the first type is given here.
The second type will be covered in a later lesson.
3
Examine the first type of attributes
List the attributes mentioned: tahayyuz, spatial/temporal location, physical/psychological needs, weakness, temperament. Understand why they are 'fruit of origination'.
These attributes are not applicable to Allah.
4
Connect to the principle of dissimilarity
Realise that this first category does not contradict Allah's dissimilarity because these attributes are inherently linked to createdness. Shared names (like knowledge) do not necessarily imply similarity; the attributes in humans have contingent aspects.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
إذا علمت هذا ، فإن لك أن تسأل : ولكنا نرى أن هنالك كثيراً من الصفات يشترك فيها الإنسان ) وهو من الحوادث ( مع الله جل جلاله كصفة العلم والقدرة والإرادة والسمع والبصر ونحوها ، وذلك يناقض ما ثبت من أنه مخالف للحوادث
والجواب أن الإنسان يتصف بطائفتين من الصفات : الأولى صفات هي في الحقيقة ثمرة الحدوث والمخلوقية القائمة فيه ، كالتحيز في المكان والزمان والحاجات الجسمية والنفسية المختلفة وعوارض العجز والضعف ومظاهر الطبع ، فهذه صفات نابعة من كيانه الذي يتميز بالحدوث
When you know this, then you may ask: But we see that there are many attributes in which man (and he is from the contingent things) shares with Allah, may His majesty be exalted, such as the attribute of knowledge, power, will, hearing, sight, and the like, and that contradicts what has been established that He is dissimilar to contingent things. And the answer is that man is characterized by two categories of attributes: the first are attributes that are in reality the fruit of origination (huduth) and createdness (makhluqiyya) inherent in him, such as occupying space in place and time, various physical and psychological needs, accidents of incapacity and weakness, and manifestations of temperament; these are attributes emanating from his being which is distinguished by origination.
Source pages p. 117

Commentary
This lesson presents a dialectical objection and the beginning of its answer. The objection states that many attributes (knowledge, power, will, hearing, sight, etc.) are shared between humans (who are contingent beings) and Allah, which seems to contradict the attribute of dissimilarity (al-mukhalafatu lil-hawadith). The answer begins by classifying human attributes into two categories. The first category includes attributes that are inherently tied to contingency and createdness, such as occupying space (tahayyuz), temporal location, physical and psychological needs, weakness, and temperament. These attributes arise from the created nature of humans and do not apply to Allah. The answer implies that the second category (not completed in the passage) includes attributes that are shared in name but are realized in a manner befitting Allah, free from imperfection. The lesson thus sets up the distinction between essential and contingent aspects of attributes.

الحدوث
المخلوقية

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Shared names for attributes imply similarity in essence between humans and Allah.
Correction: Many human attributes are inherently linked to contingency (like spatial occupation, needs) and do not apply to Allah. Even shared names like knowledge are realized in humans in a manner tied to createdness, whereas Allah's knowledge is absolute and not contingent.

Checkpoint

A) That humans have attributes that Allah does not have.
B) That many attributes are shared between humans and Allah, contradicting His dissimilarity to created things.
C) That the rational proof for dissimilarity is weak.
D) That the Qur'anic verses are unclear.

Key Takeaway
Shared attribute names do not imply identical realities; many human attributes are rooted in createdness and cannot be attributed to Allah.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?

?
The passage shows that many human attributes are rooted in createdness and limitation. How often do you project your own limited understanding of power or mercy onto Allah, expecting Him to act within your categories? Repent of that and commit to studying His attributes as He has described them.

Why Divine Overflow Does Not Imply Partnership
Buti explains why attributes given to humans as divine overflow do not imply partnership with God.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why the attributes that God grants to humans as limited effusions do not make humans partners with God, and how these attributes differ in essence and reality.

Core Idea
Divine overflow gives humans shadows of God's attributes, not partnership.

In this passage, Buti addresses a potential confusion: if humans possess attributes like knowledge and power, does that make them like God? He explains that these attributes are merely effusions from God, not inherent to humans, and that they share only the name, not the reality. The passage provides two key reasons that safeguard divine transcendence.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once without analysis, then read the translation. Notice how Buti introduces the second category of attributes and then gives two reasons.
The passage is dense; take your time.
2
Identify the two reasons
Look for the points where Buti says 'first' and 'second'. Understand each reason: why essential vs. non-essential, and why difference in reality and essence matters.
The reasons are explicitly numbered in the Arabic.
3
Reflect on the conclusion
Read the concluding sentence about the negation of likeness. What does the negation apply to, and what does it exclude?
4
Connect to the bigger picture
Think about how this argument fits into the broader discussion of divine attributes and transcendence. Why is it important to distinguish between essential and bestowed attributes?
This relates to the core idea of the lesson.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
والطائفة الثانية صفات هي في الحقيقة من صفات الله جل جلاله ، ولكنه سبحانه وتعالى متع الإنسان بفيوضات يسيرة جداً منها ، ليتهيأ له بواسطتها أن ينهض بالتكاليف التي خلق من أجلها ، وليتسنى له أن يسخر لنفسه مظاهر الكون التي من حوله ويفيد منها ، كما مر بيان ذلك في التمهيد الثاني لهذا الكتاب ، كالعلم والقدرة والإرادة والإدراك وما شابه ذلك ، فهذه الصفات ليست نابعة من كيانه المتميز بالحدوث ، بل هي ليست منها في شيء ، وليست من خصائصه مطلقاً . وبتعبير آخر نقول : إن القدر اليسير الذي يتمتع الإنسان بـه من هذه الصفات لا يسوغ اعتبار الإنسان شريكاً مع الله فيها لسببين : الأول - أنها صفات ذاتية بالنسبة الله تعالى ، أما بالنسبة للإنسان فهي صفات غير ذاتية ، إذ هي في حقيقتها ليست أكثر من فيــوضـات إلهية عليـه وهيهات أن يكون هذا المعنى موجباً لشركة الإنسان مع الله في شيء منها الثاني - أنها تختلف عن صفات الله تعالى في الحقيقة والجوهر ، وإنما تشترك معها في التسمية فقط ، ولولا التجاوز في الإطلاق وملاحظة الاصطلاحات الخاصة بالإنسان لما استقام الاشتراك في التسمية أيضاً ، إذ ما هي قيمة العلم الذي يتصف به الإنسان أمام علم الله تعالى ، وما هي قيمة القوة التي قد يتمتع بها الإنسان في جنب قوة الله وعظيم سلطانه ؟.. والخلاصة أن الملاحظ في نفي مماثلة الله تعالى للحوادث ، نفي الماثلة في الصفات التي هي من مستلزمات الحدوث وخصائصه ، أما الصفات الأخرى التي هي من مستلزمات الرب جل جلاله ، ولكنه سبحانه وتعالى أفاض منها آثاراً أو ظلالاً على بعض مخلوقاته كالإنسان ، فهي غير داخلة في عموم هذا النفي
The second category are attributes which are in reality from the attributes of God, Exalted and Majestic is He, but He, glorified and exalted, has granted man very slight effusions from them, so that by means of them he may be enabled to undertake the obligations for which he was created, and so that he may be able to subdue for himself the phenomena of the universe around him and benefit from them, as was explained in the second introduction of this book, such as knowledge, power, will, perception, and the like. So these attributes are not derived from his being distinguished by temporality, rather they are not from it at all, and they are absolutely not among his characteristics. In other words, we say: the small amount that man enjoys of these attributes does not justify considering man a partner with God in them for two reasons: First – they are essential attributes with respect to God Almighty, but with respect to man they are non-essential attributes, since in reality they are nothing more than divine effusions upon him, and how far is it that this meaning should cause man's partnership with God in any of them. Second – they differ from the attributes of God Almighty in reality and essence, and they share with them only in name, and were it not for the looseness of application and consideration of the particular terminologies of man, the sharing in name would also not be correct, for what is the value of the knowledge that man possesses before the knowledge of God Almighty, and what is the value of the power that man may enjoy in comparison to the power of God and His great authority?.. The conclusion is that what is observed in negating the likeness of God Almighty to temporal things is the negation of likeness in those attributes that are necessities and characteristics of temporality. As for the other attributes that are necessities of the Lord, Exalted and Majestic is He, but He, glorified and exalted, has poured forth from them effects or shadows upon some of His creatures like man, they are not included in the generality of this negation.
Source pages p. 118

Commentary
This passage explains the second category of attributes: those that are truly God's but which He bestows on humans in a very limited way, like knowledge, power, will, and perception. The author first states that these attributes come from God to enable humans to fulfill their obligations and to utilize the universe. Then he gives two reasons why this does not make humans partners with God. First, these attributes are essential only to God, not to humans; for humans they are merely divine effusions. Second, even the names are only shared loosely because the reality and essence of these attributes in humans are incomparably inferior to God's. The passage concludes by clarifying that the negation of likeness between God and temporal things applies only to attributes that are necessitated by temporality, not to these God-given attributes which are derived from God's own attributes.

Key Terms
الصفات الذاتية
الصفات غير الذاتية
الفيوضات الإلهية
مماثلة الله للحوادث

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some think that because humans possess attributes like knowledge and power, they are similar to God in those attributes.
Correction: Buti explains that human attributes are only shadows of God's attributes, differing in essence and reality, and are merely effusions from God, not inherent to humans.

Checkpoint
Why does the author say that humans' possession of attributes like knowledge and power does not make them partners with God?
A) Because humans do not actually have these attributes.
B) Because these attributes are not essential to humans and differ in essence from God's.
C) Because God created humans without any attributes.
D) Because humans are not required to use these attributes.

Key Takeaway
The attributes humans receive from God are only shadows of His attributes, differing in essence and reality, and thus do not imply any partnership or similarity.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Do you truly live as if your knowledge, power, and abilities are borrowed gifts from God, not your own? When you achieve success, do you feel genuine humility or silent pride? What would change in your daily attitude if you internalized that your intellect is merely a shadow of His?
?
In moments of strength or influence, do you ever act as if you are a mini-partner with God, owning your capacities? How would your decisions today be different if you remembered that even the word 'power' applied to you is a concession, not a reality?
?
Is there an area of your life where you subconsciously believe you are self-sufficient, relying on your own will or perception? If you truly accepted that these are effusions from God alone, what specific act of dependence would you practice today?

Attributes of Meanings and the Mu'tazila Debate
Buti defines attributes of meanings and refutes Mu'tazila objections.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand what attributes of meanings are and why the Sunni school affirms them despite Mu'tazila objections.

Core Idea
Attributes of meanings are real qualities of God that do not imply multiple eternal beings or imperfection.

Imam al-Buti begins by defining attributes of meanings as those that subsist in God's essence and necessitate a specific judgment. He then addresses the Mu'tazila objection that affirming such attributes leads to multiplicity of eternal beings or implies God is completed by another. In this passage, he refutes these claims using rational arguments and the Quran.

Guided Path
1
Read the definition
Read the first sentence carefully. Note the definition of attributes of meanings: they are attributes that subsist in God's essence and necessitate a distinct judgment about Him.
Example: the attribute of knowledge necessitates that God is knowing.
2
Understand the Mu'tazila objection
Identify the two main arguments of the Mu'tazila: (1) affirming attributes implies multiple eternal beings, and (2) it implies God is incomplete without them.
These are presented as the reasons for their denial.
3
Follow the refutation
Work through Buti's responses: multiplicity of attributes is not multiplicity of essences; attributing knowledge does not mean need; the Quran itself attributes knowledge to God.
Focus on the logical distinctions he makes.
4
Consider the broader evidence
Notice Buti's concluding argument: the Sunni position is supported by the majority of Muslims, sound reason, revelation, and prophetic command.
This reinforces the authority of the Sunni stance.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ج - صفات المعاني ، والصفات المعنوية ونبدأ بصفات المعاني فنقول : هي كل صفة قائمة بذاته سبحانه وتعالى ، تستلزم حكماً معيناً له ، كصفة العلم مثلاً فهي تستلزم أن يكون المتصف بها عليماً . وصفات الكمال الله تعالى كثيرة ، ولكنها تجتمع في سبع صفات رئيسية معينة قام عليها الدليل التفصيلي من الكتاب
(۱) هذه المسألة مما خالف فيه المعتزلة جمهور المسلمين الذين يطلق عليهم اسم أهل السنة والجماعة ، فقد أنكروا وجود صفات المعاني هذه ، وذهبوا إلى أن الله تعالى عالم بدون أن يتصف بشيء اسمه العلم ، وقادر بدون أن تسند إليه صفة اسمها القدرة ، وإنما حملهم على هذا ، تصور أن إسناد هذه الصفة الذاتية إلى الله تعالى يستلزم تعدد القدماء بقدر تعدد هذه الصفات، واعتقاده كفر بالاتفاق ، وقالوا إن عالميته وقادريته واجبة لذاته تعالى فلا تحتاج لوجودها إلى العلم والقدرة ، كما هو الشأن بالنسبة إلينا ، وقالوا الله كامل بذاته .. فيلزم إذا قلنا إن عالميته ثابتة بواسطة صفة العلم فيه أن يكون ناقصاً بذاته مستكملاً بواسطة غيره . وهو باطل باتفاق . ( ر : المواقف : ٢ / ٣٤٦ ) . وهذه كلها أوهام جسمها في نظر المعتزلة تحميل العقل أكثر من طاقته في هذه المسائل وهو مسلكهم الذي عرفوا به. فالمحال في تعدد القدماء أن تتعدد الذوات القديمة لا أن تتعدد صفات لذات واحدة ، والعالمية ليست أكثر من إسناد صفة العلم نفسه إلى الله ، فليس هناك محتاج ومحتاج إليه . وبذلك تعلم أيضاً أن إسناد صفة العلم إليه تعالى لا يعني استكماله بغيره . وحسبنا دليلاً في هذا الصدد أن الله تعالى قد أسند إلى ذاته صفة العلم إذ قال : ﴿ وَلا يُحيطون بشَيْءٍ مِنْ عِلْمِهِ إلا بما شاءَ ) [ البقرة : ٢٥٥] وطبيعي أن يجزم العقل بقياس صفاته الأخرى على : هذه الصفة ، فيسند إليه صفة الحياة والقدرة والسمع والبصر .. الخ . والاستدلال بهذه الآية ثابت حتى ولو أولنا العلم فيها بالمعلوم - وهو تأويل لا ضرورة إليه ـ إذ لو كان العلم غير ثابت الله عز وجل لما نسب الباري تعالى ذلك إلى نفسه ، ولما عبر به عن المعلوم . فالتعبير بالعلم عن المعلوم فرع عن صحة نسبة العلم إليه تعالى ولم نشأ أن نقحم هذا النزاع الذي أثاره المعتزلة في صلب الكتاب ، مكتفين بهذا الإلماح ، لأننا أخذنا على أنفسنا أن لا نتعرض لشيء من هذه المجادلات والمماحكات التي لا حاجة بنا إلى خوضها اليوم بعد أن انتهى أمر المعتزلة وطويت شبههم ، وتبينت لنا براهين أهل السنة والجماعة القائمة على كل من منطق العقل السليم ونصوص الكتاب والسنة والفطرة الإنسانية الصافية . على أن أقوى البراهين التي تؤكد أن الحق إلى جانبهم أنهم كانوا منذ عصر الصحابة إلى يومنا هذا يشكلون السواد الأعظم من المسلمين وعلمائهم وهم الذين أمر رسول الله ل باتباعهم في أحاديث صحيحة كثيرة بلغت مبلغ التواتر المعنوي
C – Attributes of meanings (ṣifāt al-maʿānī), and the qualitative attributes (al-ṣifāt al-maʿnawiyya). We begin with attributes of meanings and say: They are every attribute subsisting in His Essence, glorified and exalted, that necessitates a specific judgment for Him, like the attribute of knowledge, for example, for it necessitates that the one described by it is knowing. The attributes of perfection of God Almighty are many, but they come together in seven specific principal attributes for which detailed evidence from the Book has been established. (1) This issue is one in which the Muʿtazila opposed the majority of Muslims, who are called Ahl al-Sunna wa al-Jamāʿa. They denied the existence of these attributes of meanings, and held that God Almighty is knowing without being described by something called knowledge, and powerful without having an attribute called power attributed to Him. What led them to this was the conception that attributing these essential attributes to God Almighty necessitates a multiplicity of eternal beings (taʿaddud al-qudamāʾ) equal to the multiplicity of these attributes, and they considered such belief to be unbelief by consensus. They said that His being knowing and powerful are necessary due to His Essence, so they do not need knowledge and power for their existence, as is the case with respect to us. They said God is perfect in His Essence... so if we say that His being knowing is established by means of the attribute of knowledge in Him, then it would follow that He is deficient in His Essence and completing Himself by means of another. That is false by consensus. (Ref: al-Mawāqif 2/346). All these are illusions which, in the view of the Muʿtazila, impose on the intellect more than its capacity in these matters, and this is their well-known methodology. The impossible in the multiplicity of eternal beings is the multiplicity of eternal essences, not the multiplicity of attributes for one essence. And being knowing is nothing more than attributing the attribute of knowledge itself to God, so there is no need and no thing needed. By this you also know that attributing the attribute of knowledge to Him, exalted, does not mean that He is completed by another. Sufficient for us as proof in this regard is that God Almighty has attributed the attribute of knowledge to His Essence when He said, “And they encompass not a thing of His knowledge except what He wills” (al-Baqara 255). Naturally, the intellect decisively analogizes His other attributes to this attribute, so it attributes to Him the attribute of life, power, hearing, sight, etc. The evidence from this verse is established even if we interpret ‘knowledge’ therein as ‘the known’—an interpretation that is unnecessary—for if knowledge were not established for God Almighty, the Creator would not have attributed that to Himself, nor would He have expressed the known by it. So the expression of knowledge for the known is a branch of the correctness of attributing knowledge to Him, exalted. We did not wish to insert this dispute raised by the Muʿtazila into the main body of the book, being content with this hint, because we have undertaken not to engage in any of these controversies and wranglings which we have no need to enter into today after the affair of the Muʿtazila has ended and their doubts have been folded up, and the proofs of Ahl al-Sunna wa al-Jamāʿa have become clear to us, based on both sound rational logic and the texts of the Book and Sunna and pure human instinct. Indeed, the strongest proof that affirms that the truth is on their side is that they, from the era of the Companions to the present day, have constituted the vast majority of Muslims and their scholars, and they are the ones whom the Messenger of God (peace be upon him) commanded us to follow in many authentic hadiths that have reached the level of collective consensus (tawātur maʿnawī).
Source pages p. 119

Commentary
This passage defines the 'attributes of meanings' (ṣifāt al-maʿānī) and then defends them against the Muʿtazila objection. First, the definition: attributes of meanings are those that subsist in God's essence and necessitate a specific judgment, like the attribute of knowledge which necessitates that God is knowing. The author notes that God's perfect attributes are many but concentrate on seven principal ones, evidenced by revelation. Then a long footnote addresses the Muʿtazila denial of these attributes. The Muʿtazila argued that affirming such attributes implies multiple eternal beings (taʿaddud al-qudamāʾ) and that God is perfect in His essence and does not need attributes. The author refutes: multiplicity of attributes for one essence does not entail multiplicity of eternal essences; attributing knowledge to God does not mean He is completed by another; and the Quran itself attributes knowledge to God (2:255). The author also argues that even if 'knowledge' is interpreted as 'the known', the attribution still implies the attribute. He concludes that the Sunni position is supported by reason, revelation, and the fact that they have always been the overwhelming majority, following the Prophet's command.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Affirming attributes of meanings for God implies that there are multiple eternal beings or that God is incomplete and needs attributes to be perfect.
Correction: Attributes are not separate entities; they are aspects of one essence. Multiplicity of attributes does not multiply essences, and God's perfection includes attributing knowledge, power, etc., without implying need.

Checkpoint
What is the Mu'tazila's main objection to the attributes of meanings?
A) They deny the existence of God's attributes entirely.
B) They claim affirming attributes leads to multiplicity of eternal beings and implies God is incomplete.
C) They think attributes are only metaphorical.
D) They believe attributes are created.

Key Takeaway
Attributes of meanings are real divine attributes that do not compromise God's oneness or perfection, as supported by reason and revelation.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you have doubts about a divine attribute because you cannot fully grasp it, are you tempted to rationalize it away like the Mu'tazila did? Which attribute do you most struggle to accept as real, and how can you humbly submit to the Quranic description despite your intellectual discomfort?
?
The Mu'tazila thought that affirming attributes meant God was imperfect and needed completion. Do you ever subconsciously treat God's attributes as if they are separate things that 'complete' Him? How does the truth that His attributes are one with His essence change how you relate to His names and qualities?
?
If you believe that God's knowledge is real and not just a metaphor, how should that affect your awareness that He knows every detail of your life—even the sins you hide? Does this truth fill you with fear, or with comfort? What needs to change in your heart so that you live consistently with this reality?

Divine Attributes
intermediate
8 min
The Mu'tazila Denial and Its Refutation
Buti refutes the Mu'tazila denial of attributes of meaning.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the Mu'tazila argument against divine attributes of meaning and Buti's refutation based on the distinction between multiple essences and multiple attributes.

Core Idea
Attributes are not additional eternal entities; they are aspects of one eternal essence.

The Mu'tazila famously denied that Allah has distinct attributes like knowledge and power, fearing that this would imply multiple eternal beings. In this passage, Buti explains their reasoning and then refutes it by clarifying that the multiplicity of attributes does not entail multiplicity of essences.

Guided Path
1
Read the Mu'tazila position
Identify the Mu'tazila claim that Allah is knowing without an attribute of knowledge, and powerful without an attribute of power.
Notice their fear of 'taddud al-qudama' (multiplicity of eternal entities).
2
Understand their rationale
They argued that attributing essential attributes to Allah would necessitate multiple eternal beings, which is kufr.
3
Examine Buti's refutation
Buti distinguishes between multiple eternal essences and multiple attributes of a single essence. The impossibility applies only to the former.
He also rejects the claim that attributes imply need or imperfection.
4
Reflect on the distinction
Consider how this distinction resolves the Mu'tazila concern without denying attributes.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
هذه المسألة مما خالف فيه المعتزلة جمهور المسلمين الذين يطلق عليهم اسم أهل السنة والجماعة ، فقد أنكروا وجود صفات المعاني هذه ، وذهبوا إلى أن الله تعالى عالم بدون أن يتصف بشيء اسمه العلم ، وقادر بدون أن تسند إليه صفة اسمها القدرة ، وإنما حملهم على هذا ، تصور أن إسناد هذه الصفة الذاتية إلى الله تعالى يستلزم تعدد القدماء بقدر تعدد هذه الصفات، واعتقاده كفر بالاتفاق ، وقالوا إن عالميته وقادريته واجبة لذاته تعالى فلا تحتاج لوجودها إلى العلم والقدرة ، كما هو الشأن بالنسبة إلينا ، وقالوا الله كامل بذاته .. فيلزم إذا قلنا إن عالميته ثابتة بواسطة صفة العلم فيه أن يكون ناقصاً بذاته مستكملاً بواسطة غيره . وهو باطل باتفاق . ( ر : المواقف : ٢ / ٣٤٦ ) . وهذه كلها أوهام جسمها في نظر المعتزلة تحميل العقل أكثر من طاقته في هذه المسائل وهـو مسلكهم الذي عرفوا به. فالمحال في تعدد القدماء أن تتعدد الذوات القديمة لا أن تتعدد صفات لذات واحدة ، والعالمية ليست أكثر من إسناد صفة العلم نفسه إلى الله ، فليس هناك محتاج ومحتاج إليه . وبذلك تعلم أيضاً أن إسناد صفة العلم إليه تعالى لا يعني استكماله بغيره . وحسبنا دليلاً في هذا الصدد أن الله تعالى قد أسند إلى ذاته صفة العلم إذ قال : ﴿ وَلا يُحيطون بشَيْءٍ مِنْ عِلْمِهِ إلا بما شاءَ ) [ البقرة : ٢٥٥] وطبيعي أن يجزم العقل بقياس صفاته الأخرى على : هذه الصفة ، فيسند إليه صفة الحياة والقدرة والسمع والبصر .. الخ . والاستدلال بهذه الآية ثابت حتى ولو أولنا العلم فيها بالمعلوم - وهو تأويل لا ضرورة إليه ـ إذ لو كان العلم غير ثابت الله عز وجل لما نسب الباري تعالى ذلك إلى نفسه ، ولما عبر به عن المعلوم . فالتعبير بالعلم عن المعلوم فرع عن صحة نسبة العلم إليه تعالى ولم نشأ أن نقحم هذا النزاع الذي أثاره المعتزلة في صلب الكتاب ، مكتفين بهذا الإلماح ، لأننا أخذنا على أنفسنا أن لا نتعرض لشيء من هذه المجادلات والمماحكات التي لا حاجة بنا إلى خوضها اليوم بعد أن انتهى أمر المعتزلة وطويت شبههم ، وتبينت لنا براهين أهل السنة والجماعة القائمة على كل من منطق العقل السليم ونصوص الكتاب والسنة والفطرة الإنسانية الصافية . على أن أقوى البراهين التي تؤكد أن الحق إلى جانبهم أنهم كانوا منذ عصر الصحابة إلى يومنا هذا يشكلون السواد الأعظم من المسلمين وعلمائهم وهم الذين أمر رسول الله ل باتباعهم في أحاديث صحيحة كثيرة بلغت مبلغ التواتر المعنوي
This issue is one in which the Mu'tazila opposed the majority of Muslims who are called Ahl al-Sunnah wa al-Jamāʿah. They denied the existence of these attributes of meaning, and held that Allah is knowing without being described with anything called knowledge, and powerful without attributing to Him an attribute called power. What led them to this was the notion that attributing this essential attribute to Allah entails a multiplicity of eternal entities equal to the multiplicity of these attributes—a belief that is unanimously considered disbelief. They said: His being knowing and powerful is necessary by His essence, so they do not need knowledge and power for their existence, as is the case with us. They said: Allah is perfect in His essence, so if we say that His being knowing is established through the attribute of knowledge in Him, then He would be deficient in His essence and perfected by something else—which is false by consensus. (See: al-Mawāqif, 2/346). These are all illusions whose body in the view of the Mu'tazila is burdening the intellect beyond its capacity in these matters, and this is their approach by which they are known. The impossibility in the multiplicity of eternal entities is that the eternal essences themselves be multiple, not that there be multiple attributes for a single essence. And being knowing is nothing more than attributing the attribute of knowledge itself to Allah, so there is no needy and no needful. By this you also know that attributing the attribute of knowledge to Him does not mean His perfection by other than Him. Sufficient proof for us in this regard is that Allah has attributed the attribute of knowledge to His essence when He said: 'And they do not encompass anything of His knowledge except what He wills' (al-Baqarah 2:255). And it is natural for the intellect to judge by analogy of His other attributes to this attribute, so it attributes to Him the attributes of life, power, hearing, sight, etc. And the inference by this verse is established even if we interpret 'knowledge' as 'the known'—an interpretation that is unnecessary—because if knowledge were not established for Allah, the Creator would not have attributed it to Himself, nor would He have expressed the known by it. For the expression 'knowledge' for 'the known' is a branch of the correctness of attributing knowledge to Him. We did not wish to insert this dispute raised by the Mu'tazila into the body of the book, contenting ourselves with this allusion, because we have taken it upon ourselves not to engage in any of these arguments and quibbles that we have no need to enter into today after the affair of the Mu'tazila has ended and their doubts have been folded, and the proofs of Ahl al-Sunnah wa al-Jamāʿah have become clear to us, based on sound reason and the texts of the Book and the Sunnah and pure human nature. Indeed, the strongest proof that the truth is with them is that they, from the time of the Companions until today, constitute the overwhelming majority of Muslims and their scholars, and they are those whom the Messenger of Allah commanded us to follow in many authentic hadiths that reach the level of moral tawātur.
Source pages p. 119

Commentary
This passage is an extended footnote in which Buti refutes the Mu'tazila denial of the attributes of meaning (ṣifāt al-maʿānī). First, he states the Mu'tazila position: they deny that Allah has distinct attributes like knowledge and power, arguing that attributing such qualities to Allah would imply multiple eternal entities (taʿaddud al-qudamāʾ) and would suggest that Allah is perfect in Himself and does not need attributes. Buti refutes this by pointing out that the impossibility of multiplicity applies to distinct eternal essences, not to attributes of a single essence. He also argues that attributes like knowledge are not additional to God's essence in a way that implies need; rather, ascribing knowledge to Him is simply stating that He knows. He provides a Quranic proof from al-Baqarah 2:255, where Allah ascribes knowledge to Himself, and argues that even if 'knowledge' is interpreted as 'the known', the attribution of knowledge is still necessary. Finally, Buti appeals to the consensus and majority of Muslims from the time of the Companions onward as evidence for the Sunni position, noting that the Prophet commanded following them. He states that he has only alluded to this dispute briefly, as the book's main focus is not polemics.

المعتزلة
صفات المعاني
تعدد القدماء
أهل السنة والجماعة

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Affirming multiple attributes implies there are multiple eternal gods.
Correction: Buti clarifies that the multiplicity is of attributes, not essences; one essence can have many attributes without multiplicity of gods.

Checkpoint
Why did the Mu'tazila deny that Allah has distinct attributes like knowledge?
A) They thought it would lead to multiplicity of eternal entities.
B) They found no evidence in the Quran.
C) They believed attributes change over time.
D) They followed a different Quranic interpretation.

Key Takeaway
The Mu'tazila fear of multiplicity is unfounded because multiple attributes do not create multiple gods.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you read that the Mu'tazila rejected attributes because they feared that multiplicity would lead to shirk, do you find in yourself a similar unease when you think of Allah's many names and attributes? Does your heart truly believe that His attributes—His knowledge, His power, His will—are all one with His essence, or do you subconsciously imagine many 'gods'? How can you train your heart to affirm His attributes without fear?
?
The Mu'tazila argued that if Allah is perfect by His essence, then attributing knowledge to Him would mean He is perfected by another, implying deficiency. Buti says that attributing knowledge is simply stating He knows. Now ask yourself: Do you ever feel that your acts of worship—your prayers, your charity—complete Allah or fill a need in Him? How does this lesson demolish that subtle arrogance? What must change in the way you worship?
?
Buti states: 'There is no needy and no needful' in the attribution of knowledge to Allah. Reflect on a time you felt that Allah needed something from you—perhaps your obedience, your repentance, or your praise. How does the truth that Allah's attributes are not separate, and that He is perfect without your deeds, reshape your relationship with Him? Will you now worship Him out of sheer gratitude and awe, or will you still hold back part of your heart expecting something in return?

Quranic and Consensus Proofs for Attributes
Buti provides Quranic and consensus evidence for divine attributes.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how Buti uses the Quran and the consensus of the Muslim community to defend the Sunni view on divine attributes.

Core Idea
The Quran ascribes knowledge to Allah, and the majority of Muslims have always affirmed His attributes.

After refuting the rational argument of the Mu'tazila, Buti provides positive evidence for the attributes: a Quranic verse and the overwhelming consensus of Muslims from the Companions onward. He also argues that even if one interprets 'knowledge' as 'the known', the attribution holds.

Guided Path
1
Examine the Quranic proof
Buti cites Quran 2:255, where Allah ascribes knowledge to Himself, as evidence that knowledge is an attribute of Allah.
2
Understand the argument about interpretation
Even if 'knowledge' is interpreted as 'the known', the ascription still requires the attribute.
This refutes potential criticism.
3
Reflect on the consensus and tawatur
Buti appeals to the overwhelming majority of Muslims from the Companions to his time, and hadiths commanding to follow them.
This provides a strong communal proof.

هذه المسألة مما خالف فيه المعتزلة جمهور المسلمين الذين يطلق عليهم اسم أهل السنة والجماعة ، فقد أنكروا وجود صفات المعاني هذه ، وذهبوا إلى أن الله تعالى عالم بدون أن يتصف بشيء اسمه العلم ، وقادر بدون أن تسند إليه صفة اسمها القدرة ، وإنما حملهم على هذا ، تصور أن إسناد هذه الصفة الذاتية إلى الله تعالى يستلزم تعدد القدماء بقدر تعدد هذه الصفات، واعتقاده كفر بالاتفاق ، وقالوا إن عالميته وقادريته واجبة لذاته تعالى فلا تحتاج لوجودها إلى العلم والقدرة ، كما هو الشأن بالنسبة إلينا ، وقالوا الله كامل بذاته .. فيلزم إذا قلنا إن عالميته ثابتة بواسطة صفة العلم فيه أن يكون ناقصاً بذاته مستكملاً بواسطة غيره . وهو باطل باتفاق . ( ر : المواقف : ٢ / ٣٤٦ ) . وهذه كلها أوهام جسمها في نظر المعتزلة تحميل العقل أكثر من طاقته في هذه المسائل وهـو مسلكهم الذي عرفوا به. فالمحال في تعدد القدماء أن تتعدد الذوات القديمة لا أن تتعدد صفات لذات واحدة ، والعالمية ليست أكثر من إسناد صفة العلم نفسه إلى الله ، فليس هناك محتاج ومحتاج إليه . وبذلك تعلم أيضاً أن إسناد صفة العلم إليه تعالى لا يعني استكماله بغيره . وحسبنا دليلاً في هذا الصدد أن الله تعالى قد أسند إلى ذاته صفة العلم إذ قال : ﴿ وَلا يُحيطون بشَيْءٍ مِنْ عِلْمِهِ إلا بما شاءَ ) [ البقرة : ٢٥٥] وطبيعي أن يجزم العقل بقياس صفاته الأخرى على : هذه الصفة ، فيسند إليه صفة الحياة والقدرة والسمع والبصر .. الخ . والاستدلال بهذه الآية ثابت حتى ولو أولنا العلم فيها بالمعلوم - وهو تأويل لا ضرورة إليه ـ إذ لو كان العلم غير ثابت الله عز وجل لما نسب الباري تعالى ذلك إلى نفسه ، ولما عبر به عن المعلوم . فالتعبير بالعلم عن المعلوم فرع عن صحة نسبة العلم إليه تعالى ولم نشأ أن نقحم هذا النزاع الذي أثاره المعتزلة في صلب الكتاب ، مكتفين بهذا الإلماح ، لأننا أخذنا على أنفسنا أن لا نتعرض لشيء من هذه المجادلات والمماحكات التي لا حاجة بنا إلى خوضها اليوم بعد أن انتهى أمر المعتزلة وطويت شبههم ، وتبينت لنا براهين أهل السنة والجماعة القائمة على كل من منطق العقل السليم ونصوص الكتاب والسنة والفطرة الإنسانية الصافية . على أن أقوى البراهين التي تؤكد أن الحق إلى جانبهم أنهم كانوا منذ عصر الصحابة إلى يومنا هذا يشكلون السواد الأعظم من المسلمين وعلمائهم وهم الذين أمر رسول الله ل باتباعهم في أحاديث صحيحة كثيرة بلغت مبلغ التواتر المعنوي
This issue is one in which the Mu'tazila opposed the majority of Muslims who are called Ahl al-Sunnah wa al-Jamāʿah. They denied the existence of these attributes of meaning, and held that Allah is knowing without being described with anything called knowledge, and powerful without attributing to Him an attribute called power. What led them to this was the notion that attributing this essential attribute to Allah entails a multiplicity of eternal entities equal to the multiplicity of these attributes—a belief that is unanimously considered disbelief. They said: His being knowing and powerful is necessary by His essence, so they do not need knowledge and power for their existence, as is the case with us. They said: Allah is perfect in His essence, so if we say that His being knowing is established through the attribute of knowledge in Him, then He would be deficient in His essence and perfected by something else—which is false by consensus. (See: al-Mawāqif, 2/346). These are all illusions whose body in the view of the Mu'tazila is burdening the intellect beyond its capacity in these matters, and this is their approach by which they are known. The impossibility in the multiplicity of eternal entities is that the eternal essences themselves be multiple, not that there be multiple attributes for a single essence. And being knowing is nothing more than attributing the attribute of knowledge itself to Allah, so there is no needy and no needful. By this you also know that attributing the attribute of knowledge to Him does not mean His perfection by other than Him. Sufficient proof for us in this regard is that Allah has attributed the attribute of knowledge to His essence when He said: 'And they do not encompass anything of His knowledge except what He wills' (al-Baqarah 2:255). And it is natural for the intellect to judge by analogy of His other attributes to this attribute, so it attributes to Him the attributes of life, power, hearing, sight, etc. And the inference by this verse is established even if we interpret 'knowledge' as 'the known'—an interpretation that is unnecessary—because if knowledge were not established for Allah, the Creator would not have attributed it to Himself, nor would He have expressed the known by it. For the expression 'knowledge' for 'the known' is a branch of the correctness of attributing knowledge to Him. We did not wish to insert this dispute raised by the Mu'tazila into the body of the book, contenting ourselves with this allusion, because we have taken it upon ourselves not to engage in any of these arguments and quibbles that we have no need to enter into today after the affair of the Mu'tazila has ended and their doubts have been folded, and the proofs of Ahl al-Sunnah wa al-Jamāʿah have become clear to us, based on sound reason and the texts of the Book and the Sunnah and pure human nature. Indeed, the strongest proof that the truth is with them is that they, from the time of the Companions until today, constitute the overwhelming majority of Muslims and their scholars, and they are those whom the Messenger of Allah commanded us to follow in many authentic hadiths that reach the level of moral tawātur.
Source pages p. 119

Commentary
This passage is an extended footnote in which Buti refutes the Mu'tazila denial of the attributes of meaning (ṣifāt al-maʿānī). First, he states the Mu'tazila position: they deny that Allah has distinct attributes like knowledge and power, arguing that attributing such qualities to Allah would imply multiple eternal entities (taʿaddud al-qudamāʾ) and would suggest that Allah is perfect in Himself and does not need attributes. Buti refutes this by pointing out that the impossibility of multiplicity applies to distinct eternal essences, not to attributes of a single essence. He also argues that attributes like knowledge are not additional to God's essence in a way that implies need; rather, ascribing knowledge to Him is simply stating that He knows. He provides a Quranic proof from al-Baqarah 2:255, where Allah ascribes knowledge to Himself, and argues that even if 'knowledge' is interpreted as 'the known', the attribution of knowledge is still necessary. Finally, Buti appeals to the consensus and majority of Muslims from the time of the Companions onward as evidence for the Sunni position, noting that the Prophet commanded following them. He states that he has only alluded to this dispute briefly, as the book's main focus is not polemics.

Key Terms
أهل السنة والجماعة
التواتر المعنوي

Key Terms from Source Passage

Key Takeaway

The Importance and Method of Studying Divine Attributes
Buti explains the importance and outlines his three-step method.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why knowing the divine attributes is essential for grasping key beliefs about human free will, divine decree, and causality, and the methodological approach Buti will follow.

Core Idea
Knowing God's attributes clarifies decree, free will, and causality.

Buti begins by stating that understanding the attributes in detail is not merely academic; it directly impacts core Islamic beliefs such as predestination and human choice. He then lays out a clear method for explaining each attribute, starting with the attributes of meaning, then moral attributes. Let's see his reasoning.

Guided Path
1
Read the importance statement
Read the opening lines where Buti explains why knowing these attributes is important. Notice the mention of human choice, decree, and causality.
2
Identify the method components
Buti lists three parts: 1) attributes of meaning (definition + proof), 2) moral attributes, 3) objects of these attributes. Write them down.
This method will be followed for each attribute.
3
Reflect on the purpose
Ask yourself: why is it important to study attributes in this structured way? How does it help avoid confusion?
The method ensures clarity and comprehensiveness.

⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ثم إن لمعرفة هذه الصفات بخصوصها أهمية أخرى ، إذ ينبثق من معرفتها ومعرفة ضرورة الإيمان بها حقائق هامة يجب اعتقادها والإيمان بها ، كتلك الحقائق المتعلقة بتسيير الإنسان واختياره وقضاء الله وقدره وأثر العلية وعدمها في أفعاله سبحانه وتعالى وسنتبع في شرح هذه الصفات الطريقة التالية : ۱ - ذكر صفات المعاني وبيان معنى كل منها ودليله ٢ - ذكر الصفات المعنوية ومعنى كل منها - بیان متعلق هذه الصفات ✩ ۱ - ذكر هذه الصفات وبيان معنى كل منها ودليله ۱ - ( العلم ) وهي صفة أزلية قائمة بذاته تعالى يتأتى بها كشف الأمور والإحاطة بها على ما هي عليه في الواقع أو على ما ستكون عليه في المستقبل · ولدى تأملك في هذا التعريف تعلم أن هذه الصفة ليس من شأنها تخصيص الممكنات أو التأثير فيها بوجه من الوجوه ، ولكن شأنها مجرد الكشف والاطلاع ، سواء تعلق بواقع ظهر إلى الوجود أم بمغيب لا يزال في جوف العدم . ودليل هذه الصفة آيات كثيرة في كتاب الله تعالى مثل قوله تعالى : ( إنَّ الله بِكُلِّ شيءٍ عَليمٌ } [ التوبة : ١١٥ ] ۲ - ( الإرادة ) وهي صفة أزلية قائمة بذاته تعالى من شأنها تخصيص الممكنات ببعض ما يجوز عليها ، من وجود ، وعدم ، وتكيف بقطع النظر عن أي مؤثر خارجي .
Then indeed, knowing these attributes in particular has another importance, for from knowing them and knowing the necessity of believing in them emerges important truths that must be believed and affirmed, such as those truths related to the directing of man and his choice, and the decree of Allah and His predestination, and the effect of causality and its absence in His actions, exalted be He. And we will follow in explaining these attributes the following method: 1. Mention of the attributes of meaning and explaining the meaning of each and its proof. 2. Mention of the moral attributes (al-ṣifāt al-maʿnawiyya) and the meaning of each. – Explanation of the objects of these attributes. ✩ 1. Mention of these attributes and explaining the meaning of each and its proof. 1. (Knowledge) It is an eternal attribute subsisting in His essence, exalted be He, by which the uncovering of things and encompassing them as they are in reality or as they will be in the future is attained. And upon your contemplation of this definition, you learn that this attribute does not have the property of specifying contingent things or influencing them in any way, but its property is merely uncovering and inspection, whether it concerns a reality that has appeared into existence or an unseen that still lies in the womb of non-existence. And the proof of this attribute is many verses in the Book of Allah Almighty, such as His saying: 'Indeed, Allah is of all things Knowing' (al-Tawbah 9:115). 2. (Will) It is an eternal attribute subsisting in His essence, exalted be He, whose property is the specification of contingent things to some of what is permissible for them, of existence, non-existence, and modality, regardless of any external influence.
Source pages p. 120

Commentary
This passage begins by stating the importance of knowing the divine attributes: from them emerge crucial beliefs about human free will, divine decree, and causality. Buti then outlines his method for explaining the attributes: first, the attributes of meaning (ṣifāt al-maʿānī) with their definitions and proofs; second, the moral attributes (ṣifāt maʿnawiyya); and third, the objects of these attributes. He immediately begins the implementation under the first point. For knowledge ('ilm), he defines it as an eternal attribute subsisting in God's essence by which He uncovers and encompasses all things, both present and future. He emphasizes that knowledge is purely cognitive—it does not cause or influence the known objects; it only reveals them. This is important to avoid confusing God's knowledge with His creative power. He then provides a proof from the Quran (Surah al-Tawbah, 9:115). For will (irāda), he defines it as an eternal attribute that specifies contingent possibilities (existence, non-existence, modality) without dependence on any external cause. This distinguishes divine will from human will, which is influenced by external factors.

Key Terms
صفات المعاني
صفات معنوية

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
What topics does Buti say emerge from knowing the divine attributes?
A) Only the oneness of God.
B) Human free will, divine decree, and causality.
C) The names and their meanings.
D) The proofs of existence.

Key Takeaway
The method for studying attributes is: define, prove, and relate to human realities.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?

?

?
Buti begins with proof. Have you ever accepted a divine attribute without evidence, simply because you were taught it? Which attribute do you most need to internalize through reason and proof, and what step will you take this week to confirm it in your heart?

Definitions of God's Knowledge and Will
Buti defines the attributes of knowledge and will with proofs.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the definitions of God's attributes of knowledge and will, and the critical distinction that knowledge only reveals while will specifies.

Core Idea
God's knowledge reveals; His will specifies.

After outlining his method, Buti proceeds to the first two attributes: knowledge and will. He defines each carefully to avoid confusion. Note especially his emphasis that knowledge is purely cognitive and does not cause or influence the known.

Guided Path
1
Read the definition of knowledge
Read the definition of 'ilm. Note that it is eternal and subsisting in God's essence. Understand that it is purely for uncovering and encompassing.
Buti emphasizes it does not specify or influence.
2
Identify the key clarification
Buti explicitly states that knowledge is not about specification. This is crucial to avoid mixing it with will. Find that sentence.
This clarification guards against a common misunderstanding.
3
Read the definition of will
Now read the definition of irada. Note that its function is takhsis (specification) of contingent things. Compare it with knowledge.
Will is the active specifying attribute.

The Divine Attribute of Will: Definition and Division
Buti defines al-Irada and divides it into potential and determinative.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the definition of the divine attribute of will (al-Irada) and its division into potential (suluhiyya) and determinative (tanjiziyya) will.

Core Idea
God's will is one eternal attribute; its distinction is only in relation to objects.

Before we can discuss how God's will relates to creation, we need a precise definition of will itself. Shaykh Buti begins by defining al-Irada as an eternal attribute that specifies contingent things, then introduces a crucial distinction between considering this attribute in itself versus in its actual relation to a particular object. This distinction will help us avoid confusions later.

Guided Path
1
Read the definition of will
Read the Arabic definition carefully: will is an eternal attribute subsisting in God's essence that specifies contingent things with some of their permissible states.
Notice it says 'regardless of any external influence' — will is not caused by anything outside God.
2
Understand the division
The author divides will into potential and determinative. The same attribute is called potential when considered in itself as a capacity, and determinative when considered with its actual relation to a specific object.
The division is conceptual, not real — the will itself is one and eternal.
3
Reflect on the unity of will
The text emphasizes that will is one and eternal; only the consideration of relation differs. This protects against thinking God has multiple wills.
4
Memorize the key terms
Learn the Arabic terms: al-Irada, al-Irada al-Suluhiyya, al-Irada al-Tanjiziyya, and ta'alluq (relation). These are foundational for later discussions.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
۲ - ( الإرادة ) وهي صفة أزلية قائمة بذاته تعالى من شأنها تخصيص الممكنات ببعض ما يجوز عليها ، من وجود ، وعدم ، وتكيف بقطع النظر عن أي مؤثر خارجي .

انقسام الإرادة إلى صلوحية وتنجيزية :
ثم إنك إن لاحظت هذه الصفة، من حيث هي معنى أزلي قائم بذات الله صالح لأن تخصص به الممكنات ، فتلك الإرادة الصلوحية ، وإن لاحظت تعلقها الواقعي بمراد من المرادات ، فتلك هي الإرادة التنجيزية . وهي على كل حال إرادة واحدة وقديمة، ولكن الذي يختلف فيها اعتبار التعلق وعدمه
ولعلك تسأل : فكيف يكون تعلق الإرادة الإلهية بالممكنات قديماً أيضاً ، كالإرادة الصلوحية العامة ، مع أننا نسميها بالتنجيزية ؟
والجواب أن تعلق الإرادة الإلهية بإيجاد شيء أو إعدامه ، قديم ولا يمكن أن يكون حادثاً . إذ لو كان كذلك ، لكان من مستلزماته أن لا يكون الله عالماً ببعض ما يريد خلقه وفعله في المستقبل . وهو محال لما مرّ بيانه . فثبت عكسه إذاً : وهو أن الله يعلم في الأزل كل ما سيفعله وسيخلقه في الحين والوقت الملائمين ، وهذا يعني بالبداهة أن إرادة الله التنجيزية مصاحبة لعلمه القديم هذا بقي أن تعلم بأن الشبهة إنما تحوم حول فكرك ، من كلمة إذ تتخيل أن معناها الخلق والظهور وهو شيء حادث قطعاً ، غير أن هذا صحيح بالنسبة للقدرة التي سنتحدث عنها . أما الإرادة فالتنجيز بالنسبة لها هو محض تعلقها بممكن من الممكنات ، سواء ظهر هذا الممكن إلى طور الوجود أم لم يظهر بعد . وقد تتعلق إرادة الإنسان بعمل من الأعمال ، ثم يطويه عن التنفيذ إلى بعد سنوات كثيرة فتسمى إرادته هذه تنجيزية ، أي ليست مجرد قابلية محضة ، بل هي توجه فعلي إلى مراد معين
·
ودليل هذه الصفة من العقل اللزوم البيّن أيضاً ، إذ لو لم تكن موجودة وأزلية فيه سبحانه وتعالى للزم عليه نقيضها وهو الإكراه ، وهو يستلزم مكرهاً ، وذلك ينافي واجب الوجود ومعنى الألوهية .
2. (Will - al-Irāda): It is an eternal attribute subsisting in His essence, exalted is He, whose function is to specify contingent things with some of what is permissible for them, of existence, non-existence, and qualification, regardless of any external influence.

The division of will into potential (ṣulūḥiyya) and determinative (tanjīziyya):
Then if you observe this attribute, insofar as it is an eternal meaning subsisting in the essence of Allah, suitable for specifying contingent things by it, that is the potential will (al-irāda al-ṣulūḥiyya); and if you observe its actual relation (taʿalluq) to a particular object of will (murād), that is the determinative will (al-irāda al-tanjīziyya). In any case, it is one will and eternal, but what differs in it is the consideration of relation and its absence.
Perhaps you will ask: How can the relation of the divine will to contingent things also be eternal, like the general potential will, while we call it determinative?
The answer: The relation of the divine will to bringing something into existence or annihilating it is eternal and cannot be temporal. For if it were otherwise, it would entail that Allah is not knowledgeable of some of what He wills to create and do in the future. That is impossible as has been explained earlier. Thus the opposite is proven, namely that Allah knows from eternity all that He will do and create at the appropriate time and moment, and this necessarily means that the determinative will of Allah accompanies His eternal knowledge. Know that the doubt only circles around your mind because of the word (tanjīz) as you imagine that its meaning is creation and manifestation, which is definitely temporal. However, this is true for power (al-qudra) which we will speak about. As for will, determinativeness for it is merely its relation to a possible existent, whether that possible has appeared into the stage of existence or not yet. The will of a human may attach to an action, then he postpones its execution for many years, and this will of his is called determinative, i.e. it is not merely a pure potentiality, but rather an actual orientation towards a specific object of will.
·
And the proof of this attribute is also from reason, the evident necessity: for if it were not existent and eternal in Him, exalted is He, then its opposite would be necessitated for Him, which is coercion (ikrāh), and that entails a coercer, and that contradicts the Necessary Existent and the meaning of divinity.
Source pages p. 120

Commentary
This passage is a comprehensive treatment of the divine attribute of will (al-Irāda). It begins by defining will as an eternal attribute subsisting in God's essence, whose function is to specify contingent things with some of their permissible states (existence, non-existence, or qualification) without any external influence. This definition already contains the key idea that will is not the same as power—it specifies, but does not necessarily create. The author then divides will into two types: potential will (al-irāda al-ṣulūḥiyya) and determinative will (al-irāda al-tanjīziyya). Potential will is the attribute considered in itself as an eternal meaning suitable for specifying; determinative will is the same attribute considered in its actual relation (taʿalluq) to a particular object of will. Despite this division, the will itself is one and eternal; the difference is only in the consideration of whether the relation is taken into account or not. Next, an objection is raised: how can the relation (taʿalluq) of the divine will to contingent things be eternal, like the potential will, when it is called 'determinative'? The answer is that this relation to bringing something into existence or annihilating it is itself eternal and cannot be temporal. If it were temporal, it would entail that God was not always knowledgeable of what He wills to create in the future, which is impossible. Thus, God knows from eternity what He will do, and His determinative will accompanies that eternal knowledge. The author then clarifies a likely confusion: the word 'tanjīz' (determinativeness) might be misunderstood as creation or temporal manifestation, but that is true for power (al-qudra), not will. For will, determinativeness is merely the relation to a possible existent, whether or not that existent has appeared. The author gives a human analogy: if a person wills to do something and postpones it for years, his will is still called determinative because it is an actual orientation, not a mere potential. Finally, a rational proof is given: if will were not existent and eternal in God, then its opposite—coercion (ikrāh)—would be necessitated, which requires a coercer, contradicting the Necessary Existent and the meaning of divinity. Thus, the attribute of will is established as necessary and eternal.

Key Terms
الإرادة
الإرادة الصلوحية
الإرادة التنجيزية
تعلق

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some may think that God has two separate wills: one potential and one actual.
Correction: This passage clarifies that it is one will; the distinction is only in how we consider it (in itself or with relation to an object).

Checkpoint
According to Buti, what is the function of the attribute of will (al-Irada)?
A) To create things directly
B) To specify contingent things with some of their permissible states
C) To know all things
D) To compel creation

Key Takeaway
The divine will is one eternal attribute; its division into potential and determinative is only a conceptual distinction based on whether we consider it with or without relation to a particular object.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you face a decision about your future, do you genuinely believe that God's will has already specified which outcome is best for you, even if you cannot see it? How does that change the way you plan or worry?
?
If the divine will is one and eternal, never changing, then your entire life—past, present, and future—is already encompassed by that single eternal act of will. Does that thought bring you peace or discomfort? Why?
?
This lesson distinguishes between potential will (capacity to specify anything) and determinative will (the will that actually decrees specific events). In your daily life, do you live as though you are subject to God's determinative will, or do you act as if you can escape it? Give an honest example.

6 min
The Eternity of Determinative Will and Its Proof
Buti answers an objection about the eternity of determinative will and gives a rational proof.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why the relation of God's will to contingent things is eternal, and you will learn the rational proof for the existence of the attribute of will.

Core Idea
God's determinative will is eternal because His knowledge is eternal.

After defining will and its division, a natural objection arises: if determinative will is a relation to specific objects, how can it be eternal like potential will? Buti answers by linking the eternity of will to the eternity of God's knowledge. He then provides a rational proof that the opposite of will—coercion—would contradict God's necessity.

Guided Path
1
Read the objection carefully
The objection asks: How can the relation (ta'alluq) of God's will be eternal if it is called 'determinative' (tanjiziyya)? The word 'tanjiz' might suggest temporal execution.
This is a common confusion: people think determinative must mean 'creating in time'.
2
Follow the answer
The answer: the relation is eternal because God's knowledge is eternal. If the relation were temporal, God would not have known some future events, which is impossible.
3
Clarify the meaning of 'tanjiz'
Buti explains that 'tanjiz' here means mere relation to a possible, not creation. Creation is related to power (qudra), not will.
He gives a human analogy: a person's will to do something years later is still called determinative.
4
Understand the rational proof
The proof: if will were not present in God, its opposite (coercion) would follow, which entails a coercer, impossible for the Necessary Existent.
This is a proof by contradiction, using the principle that if a necessary attribute is absent, its opposite must be present.

Source Text

?

The Two Aspects of the Divine Will
Defines the two aspects of the divine will: sanctioning and executive.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the distinction between the sanctioning and executive aspects of the divine will, and how the will remains one eternal attribute.

Core Idea
God's will is one eternal capacity that becomes specified action.

This passage from Sanusi's creed explains a key distinction within the attribute of the divine will. While we speak of God's will as a single eternal attribute, theologians differentiate between its mere capacity to specify possibles and its actual engagement with a particular object. Understanding this distinction clarifies how God's will relates to His creation without multiplicity.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic and English carefully. Note that the author uses a conditional 'if you observe' to introduce two perspectives.
Pay attention to the terms 'ṣulūḥiyya' and 'tanjīziyya'.
2
Distinguish the two considerations
Identify what each aspect refers to: capacity vs. actual attachment. Understand that the will itself is unchanged.
This is a conceptual distinction, not a real division.
3
Reflect on the unity of the attribute
The passage emphasizes that despite the distinction, the will is one and eternal. Consider why theologians make this distinction.
It helps avoid attributing change to God.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
انقسام الإرادة إلى صلوحية وتنجيزية: ثم إنك إن لاحظت هذه الصفة، من حيث هي معنى أزلي قائم بذات الله صالح لأن تخصص به الممكنات، فتلك الإرادة الصلوحية، وإن لاحظت تعلقها الواقعي بمراد من المرادات، فتلك هي الإرادة التنجيزية. وهي على كل حال إرادة واحدة وقديمة، ولكن الذي يختلف فيها اعتبار التعلق وعدمه.
Division of the will into sanctioning and executive: Then if you observe this attribute from the aspect that it is an eternal meaning subsisting in God's essence, suitable for specifying possibles, that is the sanctioning will. And if you observe its real attachment to an object among objects of will, that is the executive will. It is in any case one eternal will, but what differs in it is the consideration of attachment and its absence.
Source pages p. 121

Commentary
This passage defines and divides the divine attribute of will (al-irāda). The author explains that the will is one eternal attribute subsisting in God's essence. However, it can be considered in two ways: first, as a mere capacity or suitability to specify possibles—this is called the sanctioning will (al-irāda al-ṣulūḥiyya). Second, as the actual specific attachment to a particular possible—this is called the executive will (al-irāda al-tanjīziyya). Despite this distinction, the attribute itself remains one and eternal; what changes is only whether we consider its attachment or lack thereof to an object.

Key Terms
